<!--Polyfills CustomEvent constructor in IE. Allows us to use events to manage race conditions in client side js-->
<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41588"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="The genome of woodland strawberry ( Fragaria vesca )"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Genetics 2010 43:2"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2010-12-26"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2010 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="The International Strawberry Sequencing Consortium reports the draft genome of the woodland strawberry (Fragaria vesca). The genome of this diploid species should serve as a reference genome for the Fragaria genus, as the cultivated strawberry (Fragaria &#215; ananassa) is an octoploid where F. vesca is predicted to be a subgenome donor. The woodland strawberry, Fragaria vesca (2n = 2x = 14), is a versatile experimental plant system. This diminutive herbaceous perennial has a small genome (240 Mb), is amenable to genetic transformation and shares substantial sequence identity with the cultivated strawberry (Fragaria &#215; ananassa) and other economically important rosaceous plants. Here we report the draft F. vesca genome, which was sequenced to &#215;39 coverage using second-generation technology, assembled de novo and then anchored to the genetic linkage map into seven pseudochromosomes. This diploid strawberry sequence lacks the large genome duplications seen in other rosids. Gene prediction modeling identified 34,809 genes, with most being supported by transcriptome mapping. Genes critical to valuable horticultural traits including flavor, nutritional value and flowering time were identified. Macrosyntenic relationships between Fragaria and Prunus predict a hypothetical ancestral Rosaceae genome that had nine chromosomes. New phylogenetic analysis of 154 protein-coding genes suggests that assignment of Populus to Malvidae, rather than Fabidae, is warranted."/>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs2" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs2">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs2-content"><p>The woodland strawberry, <i>Fragaria vesca</i> (2<i>n</i> = 2<i>x</i> = 14), is a versatile experimental plant system. This diminutive herbaceous perennial has a small genome (240 Mb), is amenable to genetic transformation and shares substantial sequence identity with the cultivated strawberry (<i>Fragaria</i> Ã <i>ananassa</i>) and other economically important rosaceous plants. Here we report the draft <i>F. vesca</i> genome, which was sequenced to Ã39 coverage using second-generation technology, assembled <i>de novo</i> and then anchored to the genetic linkage map into seven pseudochromosomes. This diploid strawberry sequence lacks the large genome duplications seen in other rosids. Gene prediction modeling identified 34,809 genes, with most being supported by transcriptome mapping. Genes critical to valuable horticultural traits including flavor, nutritional value and flowering time were identified. Macrosyntenic relationships between <i>Fragaria</i> and <i>Prunus</i> predict a hypothetical ancestral Rosaceae genome that had nine chromosomes. New phylogenetic analysis of 154 protein-coding genes suggests that assignment of <i>Populus</i> to Malvidae, rather than Fabidae, is warranted.</p></div></div></section>

                </noscript>

                    </div>
                
                
                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Main"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Main</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>The cultivated strawberry, <i>F.</i> Ã <i>ananassa</i>, originated <span class="stix">â¼</span>250 years ago and is among the youngest crop species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Darrow, G.M. The Strawberry: History, Breeding and Physiology. (Holt, Rinehart and Winston, New York, New York, USA, 1966)." href="/articles/ng.740#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d43998e1856">1</a></sup>. Botanically, it is neither a berry nor a true fruit, as the actual fruit consists of the abundant dry achenes (or seeds) that dot the surface of a fleshy modified shoot tip, the receptacle. Unlike other Rosaceae family crops such as apple and peach, the strawberry is considered to be non-climacteric because the flesh does not ripen in response to ethylene. Genomically, <i>F</i>. Ã <i>ananassa</i> is among the most complex of crop plants, harboring eight sets of chromosomes (2<i>n</i> = 8<i>x</i> = 56) derived from as many as four different diploid ancestors. Paradoxically, the small basic (<i>x</i> = 7) genome size of the strawberry genus, <span class="stix">â¼</span>240 Mb, offers substantial advantages for genomic research.</p><p>An international consortium selected <i>F. vesca</i> (2<i>n</i> = 2<i>x</i> = 14) for sequencing as a genomic reference for the genus<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Shulaev, V. et al. Multiple models for Rosaceae genomics. Plant Physiol. 147, 985â1003 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.740#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d43998e1888">2</a></sup>. The so-called 'semperflorens' or 'alpine' forms of <i>F. vesca</i> ssp. <i>vesca</i> have been cultivated for centuries in European gardens<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Darrow, G.M. The Strawberry: History, Breeding and Physiology. (Holt, Rinehart and Winston, New York, New York, USA, 1966)." href="/articles/ng.740#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d43998e1899">1</a></sup>. Their widespread temperate growing range, self-compatibility and long history of cultivation, coupled with selection for favorable recessive traits such as day neutrality, non-runnering and yellow-fruited forms offer extensive genotypic diversity. More broadly, <i>F. vesca</i> offers many advantages as a reference genomic system for Rosaceae, including a short generation time for a perennial, ease of vegetative propagation and small herbaceous stature compared with tree species such as peach or apple. Robust <i>in vitro</i> regeneration and transformation systems have been established for <i>F. vesca</i>, facilitating the production of forward and reverse genetic tools as well as structural and functional studies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Alsheikh, M.K., Suso, H.P., Robson, M., Battey, N.H. &amp; Wetten, A. Appropriate choice of antibiotic and Agrobacterium strain improves transformation of antibiotic-sensitive Fragaria vesca and F.v. semperflorens. Plant Cell Rep. 20, 1173â1180 (2002)." href="/articles/ng.740#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d43998e1912">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Oosumi, T. et al. High-efficiency transformation of the diploid strawberry (Fragaria vesca) for functional genomics. Planta 223, 1219â1230 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.740#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d43998e1915">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Oosumi, T., Ruiz-Rojas, J.J., Veilleux, R.E., Dickerman, A. &amp; Shulaev, V. Implementing reverse genetics in Rosaceae: analysis of T-DNA flanking sequences of insertional mutant lines in the diploid strawberry, Fragaria vesca L. Physiol. Plant. 140, 1â9 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.740#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d43998e1918">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Ruiz-Rojas, J.J. et al. SNP discovery and genetic mapping of T-DNA insertional mutants in Fragaria vesca L. Theor. Appl. Genet. 121, 449â463 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.740#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d43998e1921">6</a></sup>. These properties render strawberry an attractive surrogate for testing gene function for all plants in the Rosaceae family.</p><p>This report presents the genome sequence of the diploid strawberry <i>F. vesca</i> ssp. <i>vesca</i> accession Hawaii 4 (National Clonal Germplasm Repository accession # PI551572). We achieved coverage exclusively with short-read technologies and did assembly without a physical reference, demonstrating that a contiguous plant genome sequence can be assembled and characterized using solely these technologies. Moreover, this genome was sequenced using an open-access community model.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Genome sequencing and assembly</h3><p>We selected a fourth-generation inbred line of the <i>F. vesca</i> ssp. <i>vesca</i> accession Hawaii 4 known as 'H4Ã4' for sequencing primarily because of its amenability to high-throughput genetic transformation. The Hawaii 4 accession was used for transfer DNA (T-DNA) insertional mutagenesis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Oosumi, T., Ruiz-Rojas, J.J., Veilleux, R.E., Dickerman, A. &amp; Shulaev, V. Implementing reverse genetics in Rosaceae: analysis of T-DNA flanking sequences of insertional mutant lines in the diploid strawberry, Fragaria vesca L. Physiol. Plant. 140, 1â9 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.740#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d43998e1952">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Ruiz-Rojas, J.J. et al. SNP discovery and genetic mapping of T-DNA insertional mutants in Fragaria vesca L. Theor. Appl. Genet. 121, 449â463 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.740#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d43998e1955">6</a></sup>, as well as transposon and activation tagging. H4x4 is day neutral, sets abundant seeds on self-pollination and completes a life cycle in 4â6 months regardless of season. It has white-yellow fruit and produces new plants from modified stems called stolons.</p><p>We used the Roche/454, Illumina/Solexa and Life Technologies/SOLiD platforms to generate Ã39 combined average coverage (Online Methods). A summary of the input sequence data used for the assembly is presented in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.740#MOESM3">Supplementary Table 1</a>. Over 3,200 scaffolds were assembled with an N50 of 1.3 Mb (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.740#MOESM3">Supplementary Table 2</a>). Over 95% (209.8 Mb) of the total sequence is represented in 272 scaffolds. Resequencing using Illumina confirmed the high quality of the assembly, with 99.8% of the scaffolds and 99.98% of the bases in the assembly being validated by perfect-match Illumina reads with an average depth of approximately Ã26 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.740#MOESM3">Supplementary Fig. 1</a>). The <i>F. vesca</i> H4x4 genome size was estimated at <span class="stix">â¼</span>240 Mb using flow cytometry, with <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> (<span class="stix">â¼</span>147 Mb) and <i>Brachypodium distachyon</i> (300 Mb) serving as internal controls (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.740#MOESM3">Supplementary Table 3</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Anchoring genome sequence to the genetic map</h3><p>We aligned and oriented the scaffolds of the assembly to the diploid <i>Fragaria</i> reference linkage map (FV Ã FN) and its associated bin map<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Sargent, D.J. et al. The development of a bin mapping population and the selective mapping of 103 markers in the diploid Fragaria reference map. Genome 51, 120â127 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.740#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d43998e1995">7</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.740#Fig1">Fig. 1</a>). Of 272 <i>F. vesca</i> H4x4 sequence scaffolds that were composed of over 10,000 bp (a total of 209.8 Mb of scaffold sequences and embedded gaps), 131 were anchored to the FV Ã FN map. The scaffolds were anchored by 320 genetic markers, including 234 mapped in the full FV Ã FN progeny<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Ruiz-Rojas, J.J. et al. SNP discovery and genetic mapping of T-DNA insertional mutants in Fragaria vesca L. Theor. Appl. Genet. 121, 449â463 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.740#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d43998e2005">6</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.740#Fig1">Fig. 1</a>, blue bars) and 86 mapped in a bin set of six seedlings<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Sargent, D.J. et al. The development of a bin mapping population and the selective mapping of 103 markers in the diploid Fragaria reference map. Genome 51, 120â127 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.740#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d43998e2013">7</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.740#Fig1">Fig. 1</a>, yellow bars). Additionally, we used a new method to identify segregating SNP markers through direct Illumina sequencing of a reduced complexity <i>Alu</i>I digestion of the bin set seedling DNA for anchoring 70 additional scaffolds of over 100,000 kb in length to the genetic map. Three scaffolds mapped to two locations on the genetic map and were split into two at regions of low coverage. Thus, a total of 204 genome sequence scaffolds (including the three split scaffolds) containing 198.1 Mb of sequence data (<span class="stix">â¼</span>94% of the total scaffold sequence) was anchored to the FV Ã FN map using 390 markers. We assembled the scaffolds into seven pseudochromosomes, numbered according to the linkage group nomenclature used in a previous study<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Davis, T.M. &amp; Yu, H. A linkage map of the diploid strawberry, Fragaria vesca. J. Hered. 88, 215â221 (1997)." href="/articles/ng.740#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d43998e2023">8</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Anchoring the F. vesca genome to the diploid Fragaria reference map, FV Ã FN."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 1: Anchoring the <i>F. vesca</i> genome to the diploid <i>Fragaria</i> reference map, FV Ã FN.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.740/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.740/MediaObjects/41588_2011_Article_BFng740_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.740/MediaObjects/41588_2011_Article_BFng740_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="346"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>Scaffolds representing 198.1 Mb of scaffolded sequence with embedded gaps (99.2% of all contiguous sequence over 10 kb in length) were anchored to the genetic map with 390 genetic markers. Blue scaffolds were anchored and oriented using map positions of markers in the full FV Ã FN progeny, whereas the yellow scaffolds were anchored to mapping bins.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.740/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Although a comprehensive molecular karyotype has yet to be established for <i>F. vesca</i>, researchers in a previous study<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Lim, K.Y. Karyotype and ribosomal gene mapping in Fragaria vesca L. Acta Hortic. 649, 103â106 (2004)." href="/articles/ng.740#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d43998e2059">9</a></sup> identified, by fluorescent <i>in situ</i> hybridization (FISH), three pairs of 45S (18S-5.8S-25S) loci and one pair of 5S loci that co-localized with one of the pairs of 45S loci in an unspecified accession of <i>F. vesca</i>. We also found these karyotypic features in <i>F. vesca</i> H4x4 and identified tentative correspondences between two cytologically marked chromosomes and genomically defined pseudochromosomes using mitotic (root tip) chromosomes hybridized to 25S (red) and 5S (green) rDNA probes (Online Methods and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.740#MOESM3">Supplementary Fig. 2</a>). Chromosome G displayed a strong distal 25S signal and a proximal 5S signal, whereas chromosome F displayed a strong distal 25S signal and chromosome D displayed a weak distal 25S signal. The 5S probe sequence had sequence homology to two small scaffolds that are not mapped to pseudochromosomes and one scaffold that maps to pseudochromosome VII at the locus defined by marker EMFv190. The 25S sequence had 32 matches of &gt;90% sequence identity, of which 30 were unmapped scaffolds of less than 1.7 kb. Pseudochromosome VII, with mapped scaffolds containing 25S and 5S sequences at distal and proximal locations, respectively, appears to correspond to chromosome G. Pseudochromosome VI, which also contains 25S sequences in a mapped scaffold may correspond to chromosome F. No mapped scaffold could be implicated as corresponding to the weak 25S signal on chromosome D.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Synteny infers ancestral relationships</h3><p>Genome-wide analyses provide insight into the nature and dynamics of macro-syntenic relationships among rosaceous taxa. Comparison of the map positions of 389 rosaceous conserved ortholog set (RosCOS) markers previously bin mapped in <i>Prunus</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Cabrera, A. et al. Development and bin mapping of a Rosaceae Conserved Ortholog Set (COS) of markers. BMC Genomics 10, 562 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.740#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d43998e2086">10</a></sup> to their positions on the seven pseudochromosomes of <i>F. vesca</i> H4x4 revealed macro-syntenic relationships between the two genomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.740#Fig2">Fig. 2</a>). Markers were deemed orthologous between the two genomes when five or more RosCOS occurred within 'syntenic blocks' shared between the two genomes. This analysis revealed remarkable genome conservation between the two taxa, with complete synteny between <i>Prunus</i> linkage group (PG) 2 and <i>Fragaria</i> chromosome (FC) 7, PG8 and a section of FC2, and PG5 with a section of FC5. Most markers mapped to PG3 were located on FC6, with the remainder being on FC1. Markers on PG4 located to FC3 and FC2, whereas those on PG7 mapped onto FC6 and FC1. New chromosomal translocations between PG1-FC5, PG3-FC1 and PG6-FC6 were identified, adding improved resolution to a previous study<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Sargent, D.J. et al. The development of a bin mapping population and the selective mapping of 103 markers in the diploid Fragaria reference map. Genome 51, 120â127 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.740#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d43998e2103">7</a></sup>. Our data support these same broad structural relationships, providing extensive evidence for the reconstruction of an ancestral genome for Rosaceae with a haploid chromosome number (<i>x</i>) of nine, consistent with the base haploid chromosome number of the largest group within the modern Rosaceae, the Spiraeoideae<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Potter, D. et al. Phylogeny and classification of Rosaceae. Plant Syst. Evol. 266, 5â43 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.740#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d43998e2110">11</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="A schematic representation of the positions of 389 RosCOS markers on the seven pseudochromosomes (FC1-7) of F. vesca in relation to their bin map positions on the eight linkage groups (PG1-8) of the Prunus T Ã E reference map10."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 2: A schematic representation of the positions of 389 RosCOS markers on the seven pseudochromosomes (FC1-7) of <i>F. vesca</i> in relation to their bin map positions on the eight linkage groups (PG1-8) of the <i>Prunus</i> T Ã E reference map<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Cabrera, A. et al. Development and bin mapping of a Rosaceae Conserved Ortholog Set (COS) of markers. BMC Genomics 10, 562 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.740#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d43998e2130">10</a></sup>.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.740/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.740/MediaObjects/41588_2011_Article_BFng740_Fig2_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.740/MediaObjects/41588_2011_Article_BFng740_Fig2_HTML.jpg" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="678"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>The diagram was plotted using Circos; map positions from the <i>Prunus</i> reference map were converted to approximate physical positions for comparison by multiplying the marker positions in cM by 400,000. Markers were spaced at 100,000 nucleotide intervals within each T Ã E mapping bin (see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/ng.740#Sec19">URLs</a>).</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.740/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Absence of large duplications in the <i>F. vesca</i> genome</h3><p>A comparison of the <i>F. vesca</i> genome against itself using MUMmer<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Kurtz, S. et al. Versatile and open software for comparing large genomes. Genome Biol. 5, R12 (2004)." href="/articles/ng.740#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d43998e2166">12</a></sup> version 3.22 (Online Methods) showed that long matches form eight distinct families of approximate repeats, with the largest family having 15 occurrences (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.740#MOESM3">Supplementary Fig. 3</a>). This family shows homology to a rice retrotransposon protein (GenBank: ABA95102.1)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Rice Chromosomes 11 and 12 Sequencing Consortia. The sequence of rice chromosomes 11 and 12, rich in disease resistance genes and recent gene duplications. BMC Biol. 3, 20 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.740#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d43998e2173">13</a></sup> and contains the longest (14,721 bp) of the 126 matches that are â¥10,000 bp. All other families have three occurrences or less. <i>F. vesca</i> is the only plant genome sequenced to date with no evidence of large-scale, within-genome duplication (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.740#MOESM3">Supplementary Fig. 3</a>). All members of the rosid clade share an ancient triplication, first documented in grape<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Jaillon, O. et al. The grapevine genome sequence suggests ancestral hexaploidization in major angiosperm phyla. Nature 449, 463â467 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.740#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d43998e2184">14</a></sup> and found in all other rosid genomes, including apple<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Velasco, R. et al. The genome of the domesticated apple (Malus Ã domestica Borkh.). Nat. Genet. 42, 833â839 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.740#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d43998e2188">15</a></sup>. In strawberry, chromosome rearrangement (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.740#Fig2">Fig. 2</a>) and genome size reduction (perhaps accompanied by preferential loss of duplicated genes) may obscure the signature of the ancient triplication.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Repetitive sequences and transposable elements</h3><p>In all plants studied, transposable elements are major components of genomes, both in the percentage of the nuclear genome they represent and the degree to which they drive gene and/or genome evolution. Extensive homology and structure-based searches of the <i>F. vesca</i> genome, performed as previously described for the much larger maize genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Schnable, P.S. et al. The B73 maize genome: complexity, diversity, and dynamics. Science 326, 1112â1115 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.740#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d43998e2207">16</a></sup>, identified 576 different transposable element exemplars<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Baucom, R.S. et al. Exceptional diversity, non-random distribution, and rapid evolution of retroelements in the B73 maize genome. PLoS Genet. 5, e1000732 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.740#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d43998e2211">17</a></sup>, including more than 6,000 fully intact transposable elements (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.740#MOESM3">Supplementary Table 4</a>). These elements mask about 22% of the assembly, compared to <span class="stix">â¼</span>1.3% of the assembly masked by transposable elements in Repbase<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Jurka, J. et al. Repbase update, a database of eukaryotic repetitive elements. Cytogenet. Genome Res. 110, 462â467 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.740#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d43998e2218">18</a></sup>, the Munich Information Center for Protein Sequences (MIPs) repeat database<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Mewes, H.W. et al. MIPS: analysis and annotation of proteins from whole genomes. Nucleic Acids Res. 32, D41âD44 (2004)." href="/articles/ng.740#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d43998e2223">19</a></sup> and the Institute for Genomic Research (TIGR) repeat database<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Ouyang, S. &amp; Buell, C.R. The TIGR Plant Repeat Databases: a collective resource for the identification of repetitive sequences in plants. Nucleic Acids Res. 32, D360âD363 (2004)." href="/articles/ng.740#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d43998e2227">20</a></sup> combined. No statistically significant difference was found in the amount of data masked in the raw sequence reads compared to the assembly, indicating that the assembly provides a comprehensive transposable element discovery resource. Moreover, previously identified transposable elements<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Davis, T.M. et al. An examination of targeted gene neighborhoods in strawberry. BMC Plant Biol. 10, 81 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.740#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d43998e2231">21</a></sup> and nine intact long terminal repeat (LTR) retrotransposons of the <i>Copia</i> superfamily were identified by the structure-based and homology-based searches of 30 sequenced <i>F. vesca</i> spp. <i>americana</i> fosmids<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Pontaroli, A.C. et al. Gene content and distribution in the nuclear genome of Fragaria vesca. Plant Genome 2, 93â101 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.740#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d43998e2245">22</a></sup>.</p><p>LTR retrotransposons occupy <span class="stix">â¼</span>16% of the <i>F. vesca</i> nuclear genome, whereas CACTA elements and miniature inverted-repeat transposable elements (MITEs), the most numerous DNA transposable elements, represent 2.8% and 2.4%, respectively, of nuclear DNA. The most numerous LTR retrotransposon family has fewer than 2,100 copies. Average-size angiosperm genomes have families with copy numbers greater than 10,000, so the lack of highly abundant LTR retrotransposons is likely to be the reason <i>F. vesca</i> has a relatively small-size genome. High sequence identity between some elements suggests recent transposition activity.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Transcriptome sequence</h3><p>Multiple complex complementary DNA (cDNA) pools provided <i>F. vesca</i> transcriptome sequence resources for gene model prediction and validation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Folta, K.M. et al. A.transcript accounting from diverse tissues of a cultivated strawberry. Plant Genome 3, 90â105 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.740#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d43998e2269">23</a></sup> and highlighted organ specificity of fruit and root transcripts. Analysis of the relative representation of genes in the fruit- and root-specific cDNA libraries (Online Methods) identified transcripts of overrepresented genes (&gt;twofold, false discovery rate &lt; 0.01) in the fruit (1,753 genes) and root (2,151 genes). A global perspective of the expression patterns of these genes in roots and fruit is provided (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.740#Fig3">Fig. 3</a>). Genes overrepresented in fruit showed enrichment for several categories of biological processes and molecular functions associated with fruit development and were dominated by genes related to carbohydrate metabolic activity (glycosyltransferases, pectin esterases and polygalacturonases). Flower, fruit and embryo development, maturation-associated amino acid metabolism, secondary metabolic and lipid metabolic genes were also overrepresented, consistent with other reports<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Aharoni, A. &amp; Oâ²Connell, A.P. Gene expression analysis of strawberry achene and receptacle maturation using DNA microarrays. J. Exp. Bot. 53, 2073â2087 (2002)." href="/articles/ng.740#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d43998e2276">24</a></sup>. In contrast, abundant transcripts in roots represented transcription factor, kinase and signal transduction categories. We observed overrepresentation of biotic and abiotic stress-related genes in roots compared to fruit. Results confirmed the expected transcriptional plasticity between different organs and developmental stages.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Gene ontology mapping and functional annotation of strawberry genes."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 3: Gene ontology mapping and functional annotation of strawberry genes.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.740/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.740/MediaObjects/41588_2011_Article_BFng740_Fig3_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.740/MediaObjects/41588_2011_Article_BFng740_Fig3_HTML.jpg" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="440"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>Overrepresented gene ontology categories in fruit (<b>a</b>) and root (<b>b</b>) expressed genes. The circles are shaded based on significance level (yellow, false discovery rate &lt; 0.05), and the radius of each circle denotes the number of genes in each category.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.740/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Gene prediction and models</h3><p>We implemented a new machine learning algorithm, GeneMark-ES+, which combines <i>ab initio</i> gene predictions, evidence for host gene deserts from the <i>F. vesca</i> transposable element library and external evidence for gene elements derived from the transcriptome sequencing, to optimize precision of <i>F. vesca</i> gene annotation (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.740#MOESM3">Supplementary Fig. 4</a>). Parameters of the <i>ab initio</i> gene finder GeneMark-ES<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Lomsadze, A., Ter-Hovhannisyan, V., Chernoff, Y.O. &amp; Borodovsky, M. Gene identification in novel eukaryotic genomes by self-training algorithm. Nucleic Acids Res. 33, 6494â6506 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.740#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d43998e2330">25</a></sup> were defined by unsupervised training on the whole unmasked sequence of <i>F. vesca</i> genome; hybrid gene models were generated for the genomic sequence masked for repetitive elements and marked for introns inferred from a high confidence set of <i>F. vesca</i> transcript sequences. This process generated 34,809 hybrid gene models with a mean coding sequence size of 1,160 nt and a mean of 4.8 exons per gene (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.740#MOESM3">Supplementary Table 5</a>). Predicted protein sequences were searched for similarity by BLAST against SwissProt, UniRef90, RefSeq (plant) databases and <i>A. thaliana</i> proteins (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.740#MOESM3">Supplementary Table 6</a>). At least one Interpro motif<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Hunter, S. et al. InterPro: the integrative protein signature database. Nucleic Acids Res. 37, D211âD215 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.740#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d43998e2350">26</a></sup> was detected in 63% of hybrid gene models. Based on our functional annotation pipeline, we provided preliminary annotation for approximately 25,050 genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.740#MOESM3">Supplementary Fig. 5a,b</a>). Gene clustering methods allowed us to computationally assign gene families to 18,170 genes (<span class="stix">â¼</span>55%).</p><p>Comparison of transcriptome sequence information to gene models using the Illumina RNA-seq and Roche/454 EST sequences provided empirical support for the predictions (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.740#MOESM3">Supplementary Figs. 6a</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.740#MOESM3">6b</a>). Our approach to gene prediction proved highly effective as 90% of hybrid gene models were supported by transcript-based evidence. These findings also confirm the completeness of genome sequence coverage. Gene models, transcriptional support and analytical tools may be accessed through the strawberry genome browser (see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/ng.740#Sec19">URLs</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">RNA genes</h3><p>We identified RNA genes in the assembly: 569 transfer RNAs (tRNAs), 177 ribosomal RNA (rRNA) fragments, 111 spliceosomal RNAs, 168 small nucleolar RNAs, 76 micro RNAs and 24 other RNAs (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.740#MOESM3">Supplementary Table 7</a>). These include the minor ATAC spliceosomal RNAs and the thiamin pyrophosphate riboswitch that controls alternative splicing of THIC mRNA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Wachter, A. et al. Riboswitch control of gene expression in plants by splicing and alternative 3â² end processing of mRNAs. Plant Cell 19, 3437â3450 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.740#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d43998e2380">27</a></sup>. Although organellar sequences were generally underrepresented in the assembly, we did recover several organellar RNA sequences. We found no full-length copies of large cytoplasmic rRNAs; however, there was sufficient coverage along the length of large rRNAs to produce consensus sequences (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.740#MOESM3">Supplementary Table 8</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">Chloroplast genome</h3><p>The H4x4 chloroplast genome is 155,691 bp long and encodes 78 proteins, 30 tRNAs and four rRNA genes. Noteworthy is the absence of the <i>atpF</i> group II intron. This absence has previously not been found in land plant chloroplast genomes outside of Malpighiales<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Daniell, H. et al. The complete nucleotide sequence of the cassava (Manihot esculenta) chloroplast genome and the evolution of atpF in Malpighiales: RNA editing and multiple losses of a group II intron. Theor. Appl. Genet. 116, 723â737 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.740#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d43998e2398">28</a></sup>. We also observed evidence for recent DNA transfer from the plastid to the nuclear genome (chloroplast nomads) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.740#MOESM3">Supplementary Fig. 7</a>). The correlation of reduced sequence identity with shorter inserts is similar to the pattern reported in <i>Sorghum</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Paterson, A.H. et al. The Sorghum bicolor genome and the diversification of grasses. Nature 457, 551â556 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.740#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d43998e2407">29</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">Gene ontology annotation</h3><p>Annotation coverage in the strawberry genome is equivalent to that of <i>Arabidopsis</i>, which has a genome of similar size. Preliminary annotation of <span class="stix">â¼</span>25,050 genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.740#MOESM3">Supplementary Fig. 5a,b</a>) suggested that the <i>F. vesca</i> genome maintains more genes for 'molecular function' categories defined for transport, signal transduction and structural molecules. Roughly the same number of genes was assigned to catalytic activity, whereas more were assigned for biological processes, such as transport, protein metabolism and response to freezing. Additional gene counts with cellular localization to the mitochondria, plastid, membrane, ribosome, cytoskeleton and chromosome might be due to the enriched gene ontology annotation methods employed in this study.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Multiple genome alignment to <i>F. vesca</i> as anchor</h3><p>Eight plant genomes were aligned, anchored by the genome of <i>F. vesca</i> (Online Methods). The other seven plants represent the most closely related available genomic sequences. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.740#MOESM3">Supplementary Table 9</a> (for the complete version of this table, see link in the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/ng.740#Sec19">URL</a> section) shows that <i>Vitis vinifera</i> and <i>Populus trichocarpa</i> share the most genes with <i>F. vesca.</i> This genome alignment is independent of gene predictions, as it is based on translated nucleotide sequences; 87% of the conserved regions overlap coding sequences from gene predictions, thus providing evidence that the CDSs and overlapping conserved regions are indeed true coding sequences.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">Strawberry unique gene clusters</h3><p>A total of 103,570 gene sequences from a monocot (rice) and three dicots (<i>Arabidopsis</i>, grape and strawberry) clustered together in 15,969 gene families (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.740#Fig4">Fig. 4</a>). Of the 33,264 protein-coding genes in strawberry, 18,170 genes aligned in 9,895 of these gene family clusters, with 681 gene clusters being unique to strawberry. These 681 gene clusters represent 957 genes, of which 416 contain InterPro domains and were assigned gene ontology categories. The remaining 541 are previously unidentified predicted genes of unknown function. These numbers are consistent with relative proportions from other sequenced genomes. The most InterPro domains found belong to transcription factor categories, followed by kinase domains and enzymatic activities related to fruit development, ripening and sugar metabolism. Of the 1,753 genes overrepresented in the fruit transcriptome, 92 belong to the strawberry-unique clusters and 84 of these are previously unidentified genes with no known InterPro or gene ontology classification. Similarly, of the 2,151 genes overrepresented in the root transcriptome, 133 belong to the strawberry-specific category, of which 128 are previously unidentified genes with no known InterPro or GO assignments.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Venn diagram showing unique and shared gene families between and among rice, grape, Arabidopsis and strawberry."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 4: Venn diagram showing unique and shared gene families between and among rice, grape, <i>Arabidopsis</i> and strawberry.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.740/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.740/MediaObjects/41588_2011_Article_BFng740_Fig4_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.740/MediaObjects/41588_2011_Article_BFng740_Fig4_HTML.jpg" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="482"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>Comparative analysis with rice, <i>Arabidopsis</i>, grape and strawberry genes revealed that a total of 103,570 genes from those four species were shared among all four species. In the case of strawberry, 18,170 genes of the total 33,264 protein-coding genes (from <i>ab initio</i> predictions; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.740#MOESM3">Supplementary Table 5</a>) aligned in 9,895 clusters. Comparison of the four species revealed 681 gene clusters unique to strawberry. There were 663 gene clusters unique to strawberry and <i>Arabidopsis</i>, whereas there were 262 gene clusters unique to rice and strawberry. Additionally, there were 6,233 gene clusters that were shared among all four species. The analysis was done using a total of 21 species to find the clusters.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.740/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>By comparison, 2,777 clusters were unique to the monocot. Approximately the same number of clusters were shared by the well-annotated eudicot and monocot models, <i>Arabidopsis</i> and rice (260), as are shared by the two perennial fruit crops, grape and strawberry (269), although 6,233 gene clusters were common to all four species. These data represent a subset of the analysis of gene sequences from plant and non-plant species that represent a uniform distribution across the tree of life and have completely sequenced genomes with annotated genes.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">An opportunity for translational studies</h3><p>Studies in model species such as <i>A. thaliana</i> have defined basic tenets of plant biology. The diploid strawberry represents a parallel system for testing these paradigms in an agile translational system. The <i>F. vesca</i> sequence permits access to genomic information relevant to Rosaceae, especially fruit quality (flavor, nutrition and aroma). However, only about 100 genes central to these processes have been functionally characterized in <i>Fragaria</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.740#MOESM3">Supplementary Tables 10</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.740#MOESM3">11</a>). Analysis of the <i>F. vesca</i> genome has revealed orthologs and paralogs of many structural genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.740#MOESM3">Supplementary Tables 11</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.740#MOESM3">12</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.740#MOESM3">13</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.740#MOESM3">14</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.740#MOESM3">15</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.740#MOESM3">16</a>) involved in key biological processes such as flavor production, flowering and response to disease (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.740#MOESM3">Supplementary Note</a>). Flavors and aromas arise from the perception of volatile compounds mainly produced by the fatty acid, terpenoid and phenylpropanoid metabolic pathways. Several gene families have been implicated in the production of these volatile components, including the acyltransferases, the terpene synthases and the small molecule O-methyltransferases (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.740#MOESM3">Supplementary Table 11</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.740#MOESM3">Supplementary Fig. 8</a>). Examination of the strawberry genome revealed an intact flowering molecular circuit that parallels <i>Arabidopsis</i> and encompasses genes controlling the sensing of light (cryptochromes and phytochromes) through the circadian oscillator (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.740#MOESM3">Supplementary Table 12</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.740#MOESM3">Supplementary Fig. 9</a>). Genes controlling the production of jasmonic acid (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.740#MOESM3">Supplementary Table 13</a>), salicylic acid (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.740#MOESM3">Supplementary Table 14</a>), nitric oxide (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.740#MOESM3">Supplementary Table 15</a>) and pathogenesis-related proteins (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.740#MOESM3">Supplementary Table 16</a>) have been associated with disease resistance in various species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Klessig, D.F. et al. Nitric oxide and salicylic acid signaling in plant defense. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 97, 8849â8855 (2000)." href="/articles/ng.740#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d43998e2593">30</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Dempsey, D.A., Silva, H. &amp; Klessig, D.F. Engineering disease and pest resistance in plants. Trends Microbiol. 6, 54â61 (1998)." href="/articles/ng.740#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d43998e2596">31</a></sup>, indicating that a core set of signal transduction elements is shared between strawberry and other plants.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">Analysis of transcription factor families</h3><p>Within the <i>F. vesca</i> genome, we identified 1,616 transcription factors (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.740#MOESM3">Supplementary Table 17</a>), compared to 1,403 for grape and 1,856 for <i>Arabidopsis</i> using the same stringent BLAST-identity. <i>MYB</i> transcription factors have been implicated in regulating diverse plant responses, including growth or regulation of primary (sucrose) and secondary (lignins and phenylpropanoids) metabolites in response to hormones, abiotic and biotic stress, and light and circadian rhythm. Overall, <i>Arabidopsis</i> has 303 <i>MYB</i> and <i>MYB</i>-related transcription factors, whereas <i>F. vesca</i> has 187. Phylogeny of the R2R3 <i>MYBs</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.740#MOESM3">Supplementary Fig. 10</a>) showed orthologs of many <i>Arabidopsis</i> genes with assigned function, for example, those that underlie gene expression relevant to the production of flavonols and proanthocyanins.</p><p>BLAST analysis of 20 <i>Arabidopsis</i> R2R3 <i>MYB</i> sequences representing transcription factors implicated in regulating the phenylpropanoid metabolic pathway against the <i>F. vesca</i> genome identified 25 highly homologous sequences (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.740#MOESM3">Supplementary Table 18</a>). The greatest clade expansion was around <i>TT2</i> (encoding transparent Testa 2, also known as <i>MYB123</i>), which controls proanthocyanidin levels in the seed<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Wei, Y.L. et al. Molecular cloning of Brassica napus TRANSPARENT TESTA 2 gene family encoding potential MYB regulatory proteins of proanthocyanidin biosynthesis. Mol. Biol. Rep. 34, 105â120 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.740#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d43998e2664">32</a></sup>. There are at least six strawberry <i>TT2</i>-like <i>MYB</i>s. However, when Illumina-sequenced cDNA read mapping of these genes was considered, two appeared to be silent, five were expressed, and one (<i>FvMyb33</i>; gene08694) was highly expressed, suggesting a key function in strawberry proanthocyanidin synthesis. This <i>MYB</i> gene has been duplicated in <i>Brassica napus</i> and <i>Lotus japonicus</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Wei, Y.L. et al. Molecular cloning of Brassica napus TRANSPARENT TESTA 2 gene family encoding potential MYB regulatory proteins of proanthocyanidin biosynthesis. Mol. Biol. Rep. 34, 105â120 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.740#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d43998e2686">32</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Yoshida, K. et al. Functional differentiation of Lotus japonicus TT2s, R2R3-MYB transcription factors comprising a multigene family. Plant Cell Physiol. 49, 157â169 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.740#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d43998e2689">33</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">Reinterpretation of angiosperm phylogeny</h3><p>Comparative analysis with other angiosperm genomes surprisingly revealed that the currently accepted phylogenetic placement of <i>Populus</i>, an important model organism for tree crops, may be incorrect. Gene selection began with an earlier gene tree database<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Tian, Y. &amp; Dickerman, A.W. GeneTrees: a phylogenomics resource for prokaryotes. Nucleic Acids Res. 35, D328âD331 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.740#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d43998e2704">34</a></sup> of 9,000 homology groups, searching for genes that were single copy, and filtering by a measure of phylogenetic coherence (Online Methods and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.740#MOESM3">Supplementary Note</a>), which yielded 240 genes from seven species. Upon addition of homologs from newly sequenced genomes (<i>Glycine</i>, <i>Carica</i> and <i>Lotus</i>), complex duplication patterns required rejecting some genes, whereas simple terminal duplications were resolved by selecting a single product. This yielded 154 genes present in at least eight of the ten species totaling 68,526 aligned amino acid positions. Maximum likelihood phylogenetic analysis produced a tree (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.740#Fig5">Fig. 5</a>) that differed from most earlier studies (discussed below) in placing <i>Populus</i> with <i>Arabidopsis</i> in Malvidae rather than with <i>Fragaria</i> and legumes in Fabidae.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Maximum likelihood phylogeny relating Fragaria to seven other eudicot genomes with two monocot outgroups."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 5: Maximum likelihood phylogeny relating <i>Fragaria</i> to seven other eudicot genomes with two monocot outgroups.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.740/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.740/MediaObjects/41588_2011_Article_BFng740_Fig5_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.740/MediaObjects/41588_2011_Article_BFng740_Fig5_HTML.jpg" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="505"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p>The tree is based on alignments of 154 genes present in at least eight of ten genomes. Genes exhibiting little or no duplication were selected, and duplicates, predominant in <i>Glycine</i>, were removed. Species in the Fabidae clade are colored red and species in the Malvidae clade are colored blue. The placement of <i>Populus</i> in Malvidae and not Fabidae, as found in previous studies, was strongly supported by topology and resampling tests. Bootstrap values are shown at nodes. The scale is amino acid substitutions per site.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.740/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The approximately unbiased test<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Shimodaira, H. An approximately unbiased test of phylogenetic tree selection. Syst. Biol. 51, 492â508 (2002)." href="/articles/ng.740#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d43998e2766">35</a></sup> using the 154-gene set rejected the monophyly of Fabidae with a <i>P</i> value of 3 Ã 10<sup>â38</sup>. Analysis of the subset of 87 genes present in nine or more species (38,840 AA positions) and the subset of 24 genes present in all ten species (9,480 AA positions) yielded the same topology. Resampling genes from the 154- and 87-gene sets (50% genewise jackknifing<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Williams, K.P. et al. Phylogeny of Gammaproteobacteria. J. Bacteriol. 192, 2305â2314 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.740#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d43998e2775">36</a></sup>) fully supported all clades except <i>Medicago-Lotus</i> (98%), demonstrating robustness of gene choice. We used bootstrapping the per-site likelihoods for the best tree versus the Fabidae topology to determine the minimum number of positions needed to reduce support for the Fabidae topology to less than 1% of replicates. The minimum was 5,120 positions (55%) for 24 genes, 13,594 positions (35%) for 87 genes and 13,704 positions (20%) for 154 genes. This shows a decline in resolving power per position as we included more non-universal genes, though the larger datasets accumulated more overall power.</p><p>Phylogenetic analyses of angiosperms based exclusively on chloroplast genes have consistently resolved with strong support two large rosid clades, Fabidae and Malvidae<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Wang, H. et al. Rosid radiation and the rapid rise of Angiosperm-dominated forests. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 106, 3853â3858 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.740#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d43998e2785">37</a></sup>. In contrast, studies based on the mitochondrial gene <i>matR</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Zhu, X.Y. et al. Mitochondrial matR sequences help to resolve deep phylogenetic relationships in rosids. BMC Evol. Biol. 7, 217 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.740#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d43998e2791">38</a></sup> and 13 protein-coding genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Duarte, J.M. et al. Identification of shared single copy nuclear genes in Arabidopsis, Populus, Vitis and Oryza and their phylogenetic utility across various taxonomic levels. BMC Evol. Biol. 10, 61 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.740#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d43998e2795">39</a></sup>, as well as four plastid, six mitochondrial, and three nuclear genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Wurdack, K.J. &amp; Davis, C.C. Malpighiales phylogenetics: gaining ground on one of the most recalcitrant clades in the Angiosperm tree of life. Am. J. Bot. 96, 1551â1570 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.740#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d43998e2799">40</a></sup>, placed <i>Populus</i> (and its order, Malpighiales) in Malvidae. However, this topology had either &lt;50% maximum likelihood bootstrap support<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Zhu, X.Y. et al. Mitochondrial matR sequences help to resolve deep phylogenetic relationships in rosids. BMC Evol. Biol. 7, 217 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.740#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d43998e2807">38</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Duarte, J.M. et al. Identification of shared single copy nuclear genes in Arabidopsis, Populus, Vitis and Oryza and their phylogenetic utility across various taxonomic levels. BMC Evol. Biol. 10, 61 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.740#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d43998e2810">39</a></sup>, or taxonomic sampling of Malvidae was limited to <i>Arabidopsis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Wurdack, K.J. &amp; Davis, C.C. Malpighiales phylogenetics: gaining ground on one of the most recalcitrant clades in the Angiosperm tree of life. Am. J. Bot. 96, 1551â1570 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.740#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d43998e2816">40</a></sup>. A recent phylogenetic analysis of four mitochondrial genes obtained strong support for the non-monophyly of Fabidae<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Qui, Y.-L. et al. Angiosperm phylogeny inferred from sequences of four mitochondrial genes. J. Syst. Evol. 48, 391â425 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.740#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d43998e2820">41</a></sup>. Together with our nuclear gene results, there are now two independent sources of evidence for placing <i>Populus</i> in Malvidae and not Fabidae. Consistent with this result, there are at least seven floral characters<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Endress, P.K. &amp; Matthews, M.L. First steps towards a floral structural characterization of the major rosid subclades. Plant Syst. Evol. 260, 223â251 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.740#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d43998e2827">42</a></sup> that suggest the Malpighiales share a common ancestor with Malvidae and no shared derived characters for Fabidae, including Malpighiales.</p><p>These apparently conflicting results may be due to biological differences between chloroplast and nuclear evolution. Chloroplasts lack the history of frequent gene duplications and loss that can lead to errors in recognizing orthologs in nuclear genes. Chloroplasts, however, can experience inheritance at odds with the genome as a whole<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Renoult, J.P., Kjellberg, F., Grout, C., Santoni, S. &amp; Khadari, B. Cyto-nuclear discordance in the phylogeny of Ficus section Galoglychia and host shifts in plant-pollinator associations. BMC Evol. Biol. 9, 248 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.740#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d43998e2834">43</a></sup>, especially when speciation events are compressed in time, as hypothesized for the rapid radiation of the rosid orders 83â108 million years ago<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Wang, H. et al. Rosid radiation and the rapid rise of Angiosperm-dominated forests. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 106, 3853â3858 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.740#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d43998e2838">37</a></sup>. As more plant genomes are sequenced, these apparent discrepancies will be clarified by combining the advantages of copious genomic data<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Rokas, A., Williams, B.L., King, N. &amp; Carroll, S.B. Genome-scale approaches to resolving incongruence in molecular phylogenies. Nature 425, 798â804 (2003)." href="/articles/ng.740#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d43998e2842">44</a></sup> with denser taxonomic sampling.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec18" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec18-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec18">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec18-content"><p>The genome sequence for <i>F. vesca</i> is the smallest sequenced plant genome other than <i>Arabidopsis</i> and represents a gateway to functional gene studies within Rosaceae. Like <i>Arabidopsis</i>, <i>F. vesca</i> is rapidly transformable, grows with a small footprint and has a short generation time from seed to seed, which are all traits that make it particularly useful for functional genomics research. Unlike <i>Arabidopsis</i>, <i>F. vesca</i> is perennial. Its nearest relatives are high-value fruit crops with cumbersome polyploid genomes, such as cultivated strawberry, or large statured crops with long generation times and/or spatial requirements, such as apple, rose, cherry or peach. Economically important traits including disease resistance, developmental controls and fruit flavor and quality can be addressed with this agile system. Completion of the sequencing of the strawberry genome also illustrates that a plant genome can be sequenced and assembled using exclusively short-read technology without a physical map or reference genome.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec19">URLs.</h3><p>Strawberry genome browser, <a href="http://www.strawberrygenome.org">http://www.strawberrygenome.org</a>; the complete version of <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.740#MOESM3">Supplementary Table 9</a>, <a href="http://staff.vbi.vt.edu/setubal/mapG.html">http://staff.vbi.vt.edu/setubal/mapG.html</a>; HashMatch, <a href="http://mocklerlab-tools.cgrb.oregonstate.edu/">http://mocklerlab-tools.cgrb.oregonstate.edu/</a>; Circos, <a href="http://mkweb.bcgsc.ca/circos/">http://mkweb.bcgsc.ca/circos/</a>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec20" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec20-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec20">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec20-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec21">Genome sequencing and assembly.</h3><p>The assembly of the strawberry genome was accomplished using Celera Assembler (CA) version 5.3 as the primary assembly engine. Input data are summarized in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.740#MOESM3">Supplementary Table 1 1</a>. For 454 reads, values in the rightmost three columns represent what remained after trimming reads, having removed those below the minimum length threshold for CA (64 bp) and removing duplicate reads and mate pairs. For Illumina reads, values in the right columns represent what remained after removing reads containing either ambiguous base calls or adaptor sequence. For SOLiD reads, the right columns represent the number of pairs where each end could be uniquely mapped to assembly contigs, with redundant pairs removed.</p><p>All 454 input data were converted from the native .sff file format to CA.frg file format. Linker sequences remaining in reads, after detecting mates, were found using NUCmer, and fragment clear ranges in input files were modified to exclude them. The CA pipeline was then run on a single large-memory multiprocessor computer (128 Gb memory and 32 processors) with default parameters, except for parameters to run multiple processes on a single computer, resulting in an initial set of contigs.</p><p>The paired-end SOLiD reads were then mapped to these contigs using Bowtie<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Langmead, B., Trapnell, C., Pop, M. &amp; Salzberg, S.L. Ultrafast and memory-efficient alignment of short DNA sequences to the human genome. Genome Biol. 10, R25 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.740#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d43998e2934">45</a></sup> to create additional mate pairs that were added to CA inputs, and the scaffolding step was run to create a set of scaffolds. A separate assembly of the Illumina/Solexa reads was done with Velvet version 0.7.31<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Zerbino, D.R. &amp; Birney, E. Velvet: algorithms for de novo short read assembly using de Bruijn graphs. Genome Res. 18, 821â829 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.740#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d43998e2938">46</a></sup>. The resulting Velvet contigs were mapped to existing scaffolds using NUCmer<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Kurtz, S. et al. Versatile and open software for comparing large genomes. Genome Biol. 5, R12 (2004)." href="/articles/ng.740#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d43998e2942">47</a></sup>. Where a Velvet contig mapped unambiguously to contig sequences flanking a scaffold gap, the corresponding Velvet contig sequence was used to close the gap between the two contigs. In addition, where a Velvet contig mapped unambiguously to a region within a CA contig, but the alignment indicated a homopolymer run-length discrepancy, the length of the run in the Velvet contig was substituted in the contig. A summary of the final assembly statistics is given in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.740#MOESM3">Supplementary Table 2</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec22">Chromosome visualization.</h3><p>The FISH methodology was performed as previously described<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Liu, B. et al. Molecular cytogenetic analysis of four Larix species by bicolor fluorescence in situ hybridization and dapi banding. Int. J. Plant Sci. 167, 367â372 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.740#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d43998e2957">48</a></sup>. Details of this method are available from T.M. Davis.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec23">Gene prediction.</h3><p>We used a new version of the self-training algorithm GeneMark-ES<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Lomsadze, A., Ter-Hovhannisyan, V., Chernoff, Y.O. &amp; Borodovsky, M. Gene identification in novel eukaryotic genomes by self-training algorithm. Nucleic Acids Res. 33, 6494â6506 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.740#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d43998e2969">49</a></sup> to generate a set of <i>ab initio</i> gene predictions for the <i>F. vesca</i> genome. The GeneMark-ES+ software tool was designed for application to genome annotation projects when transcriptome sequence is available. The details of these processes are presented in the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.740#MOESM3">Supplementary Note</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec24">Gene homology analysis.</h3><p>The Inparanoid algorithm<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Ostlund, G. et al. InParanoid 7: new algorithms and tools for eukaryotic orthology analysis. Nucleic Acids Res. 38, D196âD203 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.740#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d43998e2990">50</a></sup> was used to find orthologous genes and paralogous genes that arise by duplication events. Ortholog clusters were seeded with a two-way best pairwise match, after which an algorithm for adding in-paralogs was applied. First, the all-versus-all (reciprocal) BLAST search was run using sequence files from any two given species pairs (for example, A and B). Sequence pairs with mutual best hits were detected.
